Preface
This report is drafted in connection with the masters thesis Structural design of a building with
super-light decks, which ends the master education in civil engineering at Technical University
of Denmark. The project cover 32.5 ECTS point for each student and is a collaboration be-
tween Anders Grøndahl Knudsen and Nicolaj Damsbo Karlsen.
A great thanks is addressed to the following people in relation to the project:
Supervisor professor Kristian Dahl Hertz for constructive meetings and guidance in the project
and to have been available for questions when needed.
Head of department Karen Gaarden and engineer Stig Tomasson from MOE for assistance
with dening the project and answering questions when needed as well as providing project
materials.
Chief technical ocer Morten Schramm Rasmussen from Abeo a/s for answering question
about super-light elements.
Strusoft for providing access to FEM-Design and PRE-stress as well as answering questions
regarding the programs.
Mechanical Engineer Jonas Syders Knudsen from Svend Ole Hansen Aps for constructive dis-
cussions and input regarding vibrations in the project building.
Geotechnical Engineer Jesper Bjerre from Multiconsult for estimation of the soil type in the
project.
HVAC Engineer Martin Buhl from Rambøll for estimation of the amount of ventilation in the
project building.
The report is a collaboration of two projects, which are reported as one. The two projects
are dependent of each other and had overlapping sections. It was therefore decided to merge
the two project into one report. In the report Anders Grøndahl Knudsen's primary focus has
been on the beam design while Nicolaj Damsbo Karlsen's was on the vibrations of the project
building and analysis of ventilation ducts. Both contributors do however take full responsibil-
ity for the entire report.
Appendices are attached both printet and eletronic by usb. Not all appendices are printet but
all are attached electronic with the usb. All the project models from FEM-Design, PRE-stress
and Abaqus are attached as model les.
II
==PAGE:5==
==PAGE:6==
Abstract
A structural design of super-light decks in a university faculty building at University of Oslo
is performed in collaboration with MOE. One of the main concerns in the building design i
vibrations due to the sensitive laboratory equipment located in the building. The deck spans
are 10.8m and a ceiling height as high as possible is desired. The building is cantilevered
in both directions at the facade and therefore a special solution for the span at the facade
should be considered. The eect of ventilations ducts are also investigated by use of Abaqus
for changes in deformations, frequency and stresses. The nal design of the super-light oor
is compared to the project design from MOE which is constructed of bubble decks.
The deck elements are designed according to the vibration criteria of a minimum fundamental
frequency on 8Hz. This gives a cross section height of 480mm, calculated with a conservative
resonant mass. A wide beam solution with beams having the same height as the deck was
chosen and at the cantilever edge of the building in the decks spanning direction a beam wide
enough to span the cantilever part is designed.
The decks moment capacity and deformations are veried to be sucient and a xed support
between the deck and beams are determined with 12ø20 rebars for each deck of width 2.4m
to have the oor acting as a disc.
The beams are designed based on the project material and Eurocode. The beams were divided
into two types, a cantilevered beam and an inverted T-beam, of beams with two dierent pro-
les for each type. This was done to reduce the materials used. It was found that by xing the
height of the beam to the decks height of 480mm an inverted T-beam would need a maximum
width of 2100mm while the outer dimensions of the cantilever beam was based on other criteria.
Construction of ventilation ducts has been investigated and an increase in deformations and
stresses in the concrete is found when constructing ducts. The increase is linear increasing for
increasing duct sizes for the deformations and tensile stresses in the concrete and the frequency
is decreasing linearly with increasing duct size. These eects are larger if the duct are located
at the middle of the deck element compared to a location at the support.
If the oors of bubble decks and super-light decks are compared the super-light decks have a
smaller cross section height and larger fundamental frequency.
The frequencies were found through FEM-design and Abaqus while PRE-stress was used for
the bearing capacity of the beams. All parts are accompanied by hand calculations where
needed.

1
Introduction
Super-light concrete elements is a relatively new concept within concrete deck elements. It is
hard for new concepts to gain traction within a conservative industry, such as the building
industry, as the old concepts have been proven over several of years in structures time and
time again. This leaves little incentive for contractors to take the risk of changing their old
habits. The super-light technology has many advantages compared to the elements usually
used in construction but old habit and building codes that are written with the old elements
in mind makes it hard to benet full from the advantages.
The oors of a university faculty building at University of Oslo is designed with super-light
deck elements in collaboration with MOE. The building has several laboratories with sensitive
laboratory equipment and therefore vibrations in the oors are an important concern in the
design process. The decks has to span 10.8m and are cantilevered in both directions of the
facade. The cantilevered part of the oor is solved in the design process and the ceiling height
is optimized in the design through enhancing the transferring of forces from the decks onto
the columns. An investigation of the consequence of constructing ventilation ducts in the
deck elements is performed concerning both stresses, deformations and frequencies. The nal
design of the super-light oors in the university faculty is compared with the actual design of
the oors in the building.
The building oors are required to have a fundamental frequency of at least 8Hz according
to the vibrations criteria VC-A in [ISO-10137, 2007]. The oors are designed according to
the frequency criteria and vibrations are investigated for people walking inside the building,
earthquakes and wind vibrations, along with trac from the road and railway within the close
proximity of the building. This is ensured to comply with criteria VC-A.
A solution for supporting the decks in between the columns is designed so that it ensures
an optimal ceiling height. The ceiling height is also taking into consideration in the design
solution of the two-way cantilevered deck span. An analytic approach to the inuence of the
beam height is also consider in an eort to highlight the structural benets when increasing
the height. The construction of ventilation ducts are investigated by the use of Abaqus, as
dierent locations and sizes are investigated as well as dierent duct shapes.
The project includes the usage of FEM-design, PRE-stress and Abaqus as analytical tools.
2
==PAGE:10==
2
Super-light technology
The concept of super-light structures are invented and patented at Technical University of
Denmark by professor Kristian Dahl Hertz. Super-light structures have a load carrying com-
pression zone of strong material, typically made of normal or high strength concrete. The
compression zone is shaped as arches in order to give a more optimal distribution of the forces
and is stabilized by a lighter material, typically light concrete, which lls out the structural
shape and transfer the loads to the strong compression zones.
The use of light concrete gives super-light elements a lower weight, which makes it possible
for the elements to have longer spans.
Some other benets of super-light concrete are a better sound resistance, re protection and
greater architectural exibility. Super-light structure have a much less CO2 emission compared
to regular concrete structures, roughly 50%, because less cement is used in the production.
3
Project building
The building used in this project is a science faculty for the University of Oslo. At Figure 1
the building plan for the 3rd is shown together with a 3D model for the whole building. The
part of the building highlighted with blue is the part investigated in this project. All the
laboratories in the faculty are placed in this part of the building and it is therefore vibration
isolated from the other part of the building. The separation of the two building parts will
avoid the strict vibration demands due to the sensitive laboratory equipment to apply for the
whole building.

of the building is divided on ve oors, where the 1st and 5th oor are 4.2m tall and the rest
of the oors are 4.0m tall. The building is mainly divided into sections of 10.8m x 10.8m with
a support in each corner. This gives a deck span of 10.8m for each deck element. All the
building dimensions are measured from the projects Revit model Appendix A.
The decks entire oor is xed and acting a disc. They transfer vertical forces through the
columns and load bearing walls, while the horizontal forces are transferred through the eleva-
tor shafts and horizontal stiening.
On each oor a live load, qk,live, of 5kN/m2 is applied and and an additional point load of
80 kN is applied to the lower right end of the part investigated in this project. This part is
referred to as Sector 7 and the load is applied to the bottom of the 2nd oor. This load is a
crane mounted in the ceiling to move heavy equipment around at the 1st oor. The loads from
snow is given as, qk,snow,2.8kN/m2 and the reference wind velocity, vb, is 22m/s. It is assumed
that the solution for the oors can be applied to the roof as well as the snow load is roughly
56% of the live load. This leaves bearing capacity of the deck for the roof construction. All
loads mentioned are given in Appendix B.
The laboratories in the building are divided into three categories with dierent vibration
demands, all dened in Appendix C and [ISO-10137, 2007]. The three used criterias maximum
allowed vibration acceleration are as follow:

The laboratories with sensitivity corresponding to the VC-D criteria are too sensitive to be
constructed above the basement oor and is therefore not considered in this project. At a
meeting at MOE it was agreed that only the criteria for VC-A is used for all the oors as lab-
oratories with the criteria VC-B was only constructed on the 1st oor. If the lowest resonant
frequency for all the oors are above f1 = 8Hz all the sensitive equipment above the 1st oor
will be able to get suciently damped by build-in damping in the equipment.
The deformation criteria in serviceability limit state (SLS) is given as L/300 and both the
consequence class and the seismic class of the building are class 3. For load combinations on
the oors only live load is applied and therefore the partial coecients ψ0 = 0.7 and ψ1 = 0.7
are used in the calculations of the resulting load combination [Jensen og Olsen, 2011]. The
safety factor for concrete, γc, is 1.5, reinforcement steel,γs, 1.15, and prestressing steel, γp,
1.15. The deformation criteria and factors are all described in Appendix B.
Appendix D-F are given from Abeo a/s to calculate super-light deck elements. The calcula-
tions are not thoroughly tested, as Abeo a/s had them veried and focus in the project are
by that put in others parts of the project building.

==PAGE:12==
4
Vibrations
Vibrations are one of the main concerns in this project as sensitive laboratory equipment is
used in the building. The vibrations are expected to be the critical parameter in the project
and the oors are therefore rstly designed according to the vibration standards while the
moment capacity and deformations of the oors are veried afterwards. Vibrations could be
caused by many dierent sources such as earthquake, wind, people walking in the building
and trac from cars and trains close to the building.
The dierent vibration criterias that are relevant for the laboratory equipment in this project
is described in Section 3 and listed in Table 2.
The criterias in the project are much more sensitive than the usual vibration criteria. The
vibration criteria is given, in [DS/EN 1990 DK NA:2013, 2013], as 0.2% of the gravitational
acceleration which corresponds to an acceleration of 19.6mm/s2. This acceleration is 685%
larger than the VC-A criteria which is for the least sensitive laboratories in the building.
It was agreed, in accordance with MOE, that all decks were designed to have an frequency
for the rst mode on at least 8Hz. At this frequency additional build in damping would be
sucient to give a satisfying damping to the sensitive laboratory equipment if the vibrations
exceed the criteria, according to the project material Appendix C.
4.1
Determination of the buildings frequency
Before making the vibration analysis the frequencies needs to be determined. The frequencies
are determined by use of the program FEM-Design, in which both the frequencies for the
entire building and of the decks at the 2nd and 3rd oor are determined. All oors above the
1st oor should comply with the VC-A criteria. The 3rd oor is quite similar to all oors
above it and therefore only the frequency of the 2nd and 3rd oor are determined.
Verication of frequency analysis in FEM-Design
FEM-Design has to be veried in order for it to be used in determination of the frequencies.
In Appendix D the frequencies of the rst mode shape for three dierent support cases are
calculated for a deck element supported at two opposing ends and free at the other ends.
These are modelled in FEM-Design, Appendix G, to verify the frequency analysis. The three
dierent supports are simple-simple, simple-xed and xed-xed.
The deck element has the length of 10.8m, a width of 2.4m and a thickness of 320mm. FEM-
Design can not model inhomogeneous cross sections and therefore the Young's modulus is an
average of the dierent materials in the cross section. Assuming the cross section consist of
a homogeneous material the Young's modulus is calculated as the dynamic stiness, EIk,dyn,
of 150.2MNm2 according to Appendix D, divided by the moment of inertia of 6.55 · 10−3m4
which gives an Young's modulus, Edyn, of 20.9GPa. The poisson ratio is set to ν = 0.2.

Frequency analysis in FEM-Design
Before the vibrations from dierent sources can be calculated the frequencies of the oors
and building needs to be determined. The 2nd oor is supported by load bearing walls and is
therefore dierent from the other oors. The FEM-Design models are constructed from the
Revit model given by MOE, Appendix A.
The FEM-Design model is constructed from ground level and up. Below ground level the
building is assumed to vibrate together with the ground due to the stiness of the basement.
The building were changed late in the time frame of the project by removing the earth pressure
from the of the building by sheet pile walls.
The building has insucient strength to withstand the earth pressure due to the vibration
isolation of the part with laboratories, which results in zero earth pressure and no horizontal
support inside the building at the separation point between the two building parts. This lack
of earth pressure was not accounted for in the project. If these changes should be incorporated
in the model the whole basement should also be modelled in FEM-Design and this would re-
sults in a lower frequency of the building, as the basement would not necessarily follow the
movement of the earth.
The part of the building in this project has no clear indication, in the preliminary building
model which this project is based upon, of how the oors are supported along the line of
vibration isolation. This gives a large cantilevered deck elements at certain places and in
6
==PAGE:14==
order to get realistic frequency of the oors the column rows from the building are therefore
continued to the end of the cantilevered part at said places.
Each oor consist of super-light deck elements with a width of 2.4m that are supported by a
beam on top of each column row perpendicular to the vibration isolation line. The beams are
constructed as inverted T-beams with the same height as the decks, in order to get a large
ceiling height. The deck elements are described and designed in Appendix E and the beams
are designed in Section 6. The decks and beams are clamped so the entire oor acts like a disc.
Columns are modelled as simple point supports in the two oor models. Some rotational sti-
ness in expected in the columns supports, hense the simple support assumption is expected
to be conservative. Load bearing walls are modelled as simple line supports, except around
elevator shaft where the supports are modelled as xed line supports. Above each column
row a 1m wide beam is modelled. This is a conservative estimate of the width of the nal
beam design and contributes with extra stiness to the oors, as it is constructed by a strong
concrete with fck = 55MPa and a dynamic Young's modulus of 42GPa. No reinforcement
is modelled in the beams because they were not designed at the time. The reinforcement
from the beams would however likely give a relevant contribution to the stiness, which would
results in a higher frequency.
In order to get a sucient frequency of the oors the necessary thickness of each deck is found
to be 480mm. The dead load of the deck is 1546kg/m3 according to Appendix F and the
beams selfweight 2300kg/m3.
As for the verication of the super-light deck element in FEM-Design, the cross section is once
again assumed homogeneous as in Section 4.1 and the dynamic Young's modulus is found once
again. The moment of inertia is 22.1 · 10−3m4 and the dynamic stiness from Appendix F is
466.1MNm2. This results in a dynamic Young's modulus for the super-light deck of 21.1GPa.
The live load on the structure at each level is set to 5kN/m2, as mentioned in Section 3. Part
of this is quasi permanent oce equipment and should be added as resonating mass as it has
a negative eect on the frequency. To be on the safe side of things the total live load is added
to the model as extra dead load on both beams and deck, corresponding to mqk = 1062kg/m3.
This is a conservative assumption and the actual resonating mass on the deck would likely be
less than the resonating mass used in the project.
The super-light deck element have a inhomogeneous cross section and therefore the two span-
ning directions could have a dierence in the stiness. From Appendix F the stiness in the
elements spanning direction is given. The same calculation procedure is then used to calcu-
late the axial stiness, bending sti around the strong axis which is calculated with Matlab in
Appendix N. In Appendix N the relation between the stiness in the two spanning directions
is calculated for a length and width of 10.8m for the super-light deck which gives a stiness
relation of 0.99 The stiness relation is added to the super-light deck elements in the FEM-
Design model.
The FEM-models for the 2nd and 3rd oors are shown in Figure 3 and Figure 4 and modelled
in Appendix H and Appendix I. Figure 3a shows the supports of the model and Figure 3b

be mounted in the bottom of the oor. This force does not inuence the frequency of the rst
mode shape, due to the location being in Section 7 at which the mode shape has a small
amplitude. This is the lower right part of the oor in the model. The purpose of the crane is
to move the heavy equipment that is placed in the 1st oor as mention in Section 3.

The entire building is modelled in FEM-Design in order to get its frequency. The building
model is constructed by use of the same material parameters as for the deck elements. The
rst mode shape frequency is a horizontal movement where the oors has a low deection. The
beams are therefore not modelled as they only have minor inuence on the frequency. The
computing power was limited for this project and by not including the beams the model gets
more simplied without missing signicant precision in the analysis as the overall stiness only
changes insignicantly. The oors has been modied slightly in order to reduce the calculation
time additionally. This was done at the elevator shafts in the upper left corner, marked with
red in Figure 5, so that the oor edge does not use the same point as the ends of the elevator
8

shaft.

In [DS/EN 1998-1, 2005, Equation 4.6] an expression to estimate the fundamental period of
a building less than 40m high is given and shown in Equation 1:
T1,EC = Ct · H3/4 = 0.48s
(1)
Where the building height is h = 20.4m. This leads to a fundamental frequency of 2.08Hz
according to the Eurocode estimate which deviates 20.9% from the fundamental frequency
found by FEM-Design and 1.0% from the fundamental frequency with a realistic dead load.
The realistic fundamental frequency only deviates slightly from the Eurocode estimate and
9
==PAGE:17==
the frequency from the model is therefore assume okay to use moving further in the project.
Late in the project a good reference for the resonating mass has been found. Morten Schramm
Rasmussen from Abeo a/s, informed the project group that usually 20% of the imposed load
is used as resonant load in vibration analysis.
Some vibration examples are described in
[Suikkanen, 2012]. One of the examples is an oce building at which 30% is added as resonant
mass. In this project it was decided to use 20%, which gives a resonant load of 1kN/m2 which
is far more realistic than 100% resonant live load. The load is added to the density of the
deck in the vibration analysis, which corresponds to an extra contribution to the density on
212.5kg/m3.
To asses the eect of cracked concrete elements the bending stiness of the elements can be
reduced. This was also discussed with Jonas Syders Knudsen from Svend Ole Hansen Aps
and is done be scaling the bending stiness to 70%. If the static calculations shows that the
concrete will crack it is recommended to analyze the eect on the vibrations further as the
damping of the deck element is expected to increase if the element is cracked. This increase in
damping is due to the elements vibration getting out of sync as the stiness changes depending
on if the element is at the top or bottom of its vibration cycle.
The reduction in resonating mass was found late in the process of the project and is therefore
not taken into considered for the nal beam design. In order to see the eect both the correct
resonating mass and the reduction in beam stiness are modelled for both the 2nd and 3rd
oor and the building as a whole in Appendix K-M. They are listed together with the original
results for the frequencies in Table 4:
Table 4: The frequencies for the rst mode of a the project building determined by FEM-Design
2nd oor
3rd oor
whole building

[Hz]

[Hz]

2.06
A frequency larger than 8Hz is found for a deck thickness of 480mm and is thereby sucient
for this project. As expected, the reduction in the resonant mass gives a higher frequency of
the oors even though the stiness of the beams are decreased to 70%. The higher frequency
gives the opportunity to decrease the thickness of the super-light deck elements. This would
however require a new beam design and be a major setback for the project. The deck could
be reduced to a thickness of 390mm if the frequency is the only thing considered. This would
give a frequency on 8.14Hz for the 3rd oor.
Only the frequency of the rst mode shape is investigated and, as started earlier, the crane at
the bottom of the 2nd oor is not problematic, but it could be relevant to investigate higher
frequencies to make sure it remains so.
The frequency of the deck could also be determined experimentally by use of an accelerometer.
An experimental analysis could also determine the damping of the deck, which for super-light
decks are unknown, but it has not been possible to nd and opportunity to performed an
experimental analysis of a super-light deck.
The building has a frequency of 1.72Hz which is in the same range as the estimation from
Eurocode 8.
10
==PAGE:18==
4.2
Vibration analysis
As the frequencies of the building and oors are determined, the dierent types of vibrations
can be investigated.
Trac induced vibrations
For trac induced vibrations some preliminary eld studies are made. It is assumed that
trac from Problemveien would induce the greatest vibrations into the building according to
Appendix C. The considerations from trac induced vibrations is described in Section 3.59,
Appendix C and vibrations from the heaviest trac is measured to 3 −6µm/s.
The root mean square acceleration, which is the unit the vibration criteria is given in, is
calculated by Equation 2:
aRMS = v · f1 ·
1
√
2 · 10−3 mm
µm = 0.002 −0.004mm/s2
(2)
Where f1 is the frequency of the whole building.
The factor
1
√
2 is the conversion fac-
tor between peak acceleration and root mean square acceleration, aRMS, if the vibration
is assumed to be a single harmonic response which only considers resonance, according to
[Willford og Young, 2006, Section 3.1].
Vibrations induced by people walking
The vibrations induced to the oor by people walking are investigated by the use of [Feldmann et al., 2009,
Section 4]. The calculations are performed in Matlab and are shown in Appendix O. Three
parameters are needed in order to determine the vibrations induced by people: the frequency
for the rst mode, modal mass and the damping of the oor. The frequency is determined
in Section 4.1 and only the lowest frequency, f1, of 8.39Hz is used as this gives the largest
acceleration. To determine the modal mass of the rst frequency the total mass of the oor
and the resonating mass is multiplied by the amount of mass swaying in the rst mode shape
and determine through Matlab in Appendix O. The total mass, m, is 4.58 · 106kg for a total
area of the 3rd oor, A3, of 3661m2 and the percentage of mass swaying in the rst mode
shape is 3.1% given from FEM-Design as shown in Figure 7.
11
==PAGE:19==
Figure 7: Table from FEM-Design showing the amount of mass swaying in each direction for dierent
mode shapes
This gives a modal mass, mmodal, 1.42·105kg. The damping of super-light decks are not tested
at this stage and therefore the damping of the oor is estimated from [Feldmann et al., 2009,
Table 4.1]. This does not take into account the eect of the strong and light concrete vibrating
dierently and is therefore an conservative approximation of the damping.
D = D1 + D2 + D3
(3)
The structural damping, D1, of 2% for concrete, the furniture damping, D2, of 0% for schools
and the nishing damping, D3, of 1% for ceiling under the oor. This gives a total damping,
D, of 3%.
[Feldmann et al., 2009, Figure 7.6] is used to determine the root mean square
velocity as shown in Figure 8

==PAGE:20==
Figure 8: Determination of root mean square velocity shown by red lines
The root mean square velocity, vRMS, is then found to be approximately 0.10m/s. To deter-
mine the root mean square acceleration the velocity is multiplied with the frequency of the
oor:
aRMS = f1 · vRMS = 0.811m/s2
(4)

==PAGE:21==
Earthquake induced vibrations
The earthquake induced vibrations are calculated by use of the elastic design spectrum analysis
according to [DS/EN 1998-1, 2005]. The design spectrum is chosen in order to account for
the constructions capacity of dissipating energy under an earthquake along with both the
horizontal and vertical earthquake accelerations are calculated. Only the lowest frequency
of the building of 1.72Hz is investigated as this gives the highest acceleration. Before the
earthquake response is calculated the design ground acceleration and the behaviour factor
needs to be determined. All the calculations are performed in Matlab in Appendix P and
Appendix Q and is based on a script from an earthquake engineering course which has been
rened and adjusted to the project. The design ground acceleration is given by Equation 5 or
from [Solomos et al., 2008]:
ag = γ · agR
(5)
The factor γ depends on the importance class, which in Section 3 is given to importance class 3
and the value is given as γIII = 1.4 given from [NS/EN 1998-1:2004+A1:2013/NA:2014, 2014,
Table NA.4]. The peak ground acceleration for the Oslo area is given from [Solomos et al., 2008]
with a return period of 475 years. The light green area on the zonation map is having a ground
peak acceleration, agR, in the interval of [0.30; 0.35]m/s2. The largest ground peak accelera-
tion is used in the calculations to be conservative which means that agR = 0.35m/s2 and the
total design ground acceleration, ag is thereby 0.49m/s2.
The behaviour factor for horizontal actions is given by Equation 6 or by [DS/EN 1998-1, 2005,
Equation 5.1] and is the factor taking the possibility for the structure to dissipate energy into
account:
q = q0 · kw ≥1.5
(6)
The lower boundary of Equation 6 is 1.5 and q is thereby set to 1.5 if the equation gives a
lower value. The coecient kw is set to 1 according to [DS/EN 1998-1, 2005, Section 5.2.2.2
(11)P] as the static system is considered a equivalent dual system. The basic value of the
behaviour factor, q0, is given from Equation 7 or from [DS/EN 1998-1, 2005, Table 5.1] for a
dual systems:
q0 = 3.0 · αu/αl
(7)
The building is of medium ductility class, DCM, according to [NS/EN 1998-1:2004+A1:2013/NA:2014, 2014,
Table NA.6.1]. The value for the relation αu/αl is 1.3 for a multistory frame equivalent dual-
structure according to [DS/EN 1998-1, 2005, Section 5.2.2.2 (5)(a)]. The behaviour factor for
horizontal actions, q, is then 3.9.
The horizontal design spectrum is given by Figure 9 for Norwegian soil types and both the
horizontal and vertical design spectrum can be calculated by Equation 8 - Equation 11 or by
[DS/EN 1998-1, 2005, Equation 3.13 - 3.16].
14
==PAGE:22==
Figure 9: Design spectra for Norwegian soil types
The ground type at the building site is estimated to type be of D in collaboration with Jesper
Bjerre from Multiconsult. This is only a rough estimate but the best which could be made as
no soil samples were available from the location. The parameters in Table 5 are given from
[NS/EN 1998-1:2004+A1:2013/NA:2014, 2014, Table NA.3.3], which are used to calculate the
design spectrum.

specically the method described in [DS/EN 1991-1-4, 2007, Annex B], which is used to de-

Appendix R. The Matlab script is based on a script from a wind engineering course and re-
ned to the project like the one for earthquake.
In Section 3 the reference wind velocity, vb, is given as 22m/s and the terrain is given as
category II.

The acceleration is determined at the top of the roof. There will be no laboratories at this
location and a better reference point for the acceleration would therefore be the 3rd or 4th
oor. At these two heights the fundamental mode shape is adjusted and the root mean square
accelerations, aRMS,3 and aRMS,4, are 1.31mm/s2 and 1.95mm/s2 respectively.
For a mean wind speed of 20m/s, instead of 22m/s used in the project, the acceleration due to
vibrations at the top of the building would be 2.39mm/s2, which is below the criteria VC-A.
A wind speed that high would rarely occur and as of such it should not be a problem to place
the laboratories in the building without damping, according to Jonas Syders Knudsen from
Svend Ole Hansen Aps.

4.3
Result from vibration analysis
The root mean square acceleration due to vibrations from dierent sources are shown in Table 8
together with the vibration criteria, VC-A, for the oors:
Table 8: Root mean square acceleration level from vibrations

All the vibrations are assumed as a single harmonic response in the calculations of the ac-
celeration. This is a necessary assumption in order to convert the peak accelerations to root
mean square accelerations.
Trac induced vibrations are not critical for the building and is therefore not a problem for
the sensitive equipment. Furthermore the construction pit is stabilized with KC-piles and
the foundations are founded on steelcore piles which both reduces vibrations. The vibrations
induced by people walking on the oors are below the vibration criteria VC-A. The damping
of super-light decks are however unknown and can advantageously be investigated further e.g.
by measuring the response of oor with an accelerometer.
The acceleration from wind vibrations is higher than the critical value of criteria VC-A. This
is not necessarily a problem as the critical wind velocity occur rarely. The laboratory equip-
ments could also be damped e.g. by implementing vibrationsupressing supports for the sen-
sitive equipment if the vibrations are believed to be too sever. It should also be noted that
the acceleration is at the top of the roof where it is irrational to build laboratories. At a
more realistic oor plan, 3rd and 4th oor, the accelerations are respectively 1.31mm/s2 and
1.95mm/s2, which are below the criteria VC-A. The accelerations from vibrations due to wind
are expected to be larger with increasing height of the building, which the data has also shown.
The accelerations from earthquake is much higher than the criteria VC-A, but the calculations
are also made for a return period of 475 years. At these vibrations the laboratory equipment
can not be used, but this should not be a problem when it occur so rarely. Usually earth-
quake happens so rarely that only the damaging of the structure and equipment needs to be
considered. It may be considered to investigate how often an earthquake that would exceed
the vibration criteria occurs.

5.1
Verication for deformation and moment calculations in FEM-Design
To verify the calculations in FEM-Design a simply supported beam with a length of l = 10m is
modelled. The results from FEM-Design is then compared to an analytical model given from
[Jensen og Olsen, 2011, Section 3.2.2]. For the analytical model the maximum deformation is
given by Equation 38 and the maximum bending moment by Equation 37:
Mmax = 1
8 · q · l2
(37)
For the model a HE-A 200 steel beam is chosen. The beam has a moment of inertia. Iy, of
3.692 · 10−5m4, a Young's modulus, E, of 210 · 109Pa and the beam is loaded by a uniform
distributed load, q, of 10kN/m.
The analytical value for the maximum deformation and
bending moment is then, umax = 168mm and mmax = 125kNm. The results from the FEM-
Design model is shown in Figure 13:
Figure 13: Beam showing bending moment curve and the value of maximum bending moment
The model in Figure 13 does not shown the units but the moment is given in kNm in FEM-
Design. It is the seen that the analytical model gives the exact same result as the FEM-Design
model and it is thereby veried.
5.2
Calculations of maximum deformation and bending moment capacity
The maximum deformation and bending moments is compared to the deformation criteria and
the bending moment capacity of the deck from Appendix E in order to verify the capacity of
the deck. The maximum allowed deformation is given by Equation 38:
umax =
l
300 = 36mm
(38)
The biggest deformation of the deck is the deformation due to short term loading, which is
ushort = −5mm according to Appendix F. This is less than 15% of the accepted maximum
deformation and is therefore accepted. The deck elements have greater deformations before
loading is applied, the cases before the load is applied is however irrelevant as the deformation
criteria is a comfort criteria. If they where taken into account the maximum deformation
would be before the deck is loaded u90 = −13mm, which would be less than 40% of the al-
lowed deformation.
The deformation is only for the deck elements and not for the oor as a continuous disc, where
the deformation of the beams needs to be taking into account too.
Only the 3rd oor is modelled in order to determine the maximum moment as the 2nd oor
24
==PAGE:32==
has the same spans. The model is, beside the beams, constructed the same way as for the
vibration analysis. In the vibration analysis the beams are cut o at atriums and elevator
shafts to make sure the holes in the deck had the right size. These cut os gives some unusual
moment concentrations in the deck, because of irregularities in the program. Therefore no
cuts are made in the beams for the moment analysis.
In the moment analysis also includes shrinkage and creep. For the deck elements these are de-
termined in Appendix F. The creep is φ = 2.171 and the shrinkage ϵ = 0.015%. For the beams
the creep is φ = 1.50 and the shrinkage is ϵ = 0.030% according to [DS/EN 1992-1-1, 2008,
Figure 3.1 and Table 3.2].
Live load is added as an evenly distributed load on the oor and not as extra dead load
which was used in the vibration analysis. Only live load is acting on the oor and the load
combination is then given from [Jensen og Olsen, 2011, Table 4.4] to:
qk · 1.5 · KFI · ψ0
(39)
The consequence class of the building is class 3 (see Section 3), which gives the factor KFI = 1.1
according to [Jensen og Olsen, 2011, Table 4.1] and the factor ψ0 = 0.7 given in Section 3.
In Figure 14 the maximum principal moment for the 3rd modelled in Appendix U is shown:
Figure 14: Principal negative moment distribution on the 3rd oor including maximum moment
25
==PAGE:33==
The maximum moment determined in FEM-Design is −659kNm/m. The analysis in FEM-
Design give peak moment in a small area around the columns. This is attempted to be solve
by use of peak smoothing, which is a feature in the program to solve the problem.
The
moment is then calculated as an average moment over the width of one deck element where
the maximum moment in the deck is Mdeck,max = −956kNm for the most severely loaded
deck, which is below the maximum allowed moment for the deck on MRd,max = −1735kNm.
At the 2nd oor the maximum moment is located in Section 7 of the building, at which the
crane has to be mounted at the bottom of the 2nd oor but has the same maximum moment
as the 3rd oor. The crane would then contribute additionally to the maximum moment. The
exact location of the crane is not known and therefore it is assumed to be place in the middle
of the deck span as this is the worst case scenario. The supports in each end of the deck
are assumed as xed connections. This is a conservative assumption, because the supports
are somewhere between simple and xed connections. According to [Jensen og Olsen, 2011,
Figure 3.32] the moment at a xed support for a beam with a point load is given as:
M = −1
8 · Q · l = −108kNm
(40)
The point load from the crane is 80kN according to Section 3 and the span of the deck, l, is
10.8m. The maximum possible moment at the 2nd oor, M2nd,max, is thereby -1064kNm.
If a positive deformation of the decks is desired the pre-tension in the deck could be reduced.
5.3
Fixed connection between the beam and the deck element
The moment located at the beam end in the spanning direction needs to be transferred from
the deck elements to the beams. The connection between the beam and the deck element is
constructed as a xed blade connection. In the top of the connection the beam and the deck
is xed together by slack reinforcement. The slack reinforcement is placed in pre-fabricated
grooves which are cast out after the reinforcement is correctly placed. As the deck element is
xed in the ends, the moment to transfer will always be negative and therefore the top of the
element is in tension. The bottom part of the connection needs to transfer the compression
forces and therefore full contact between the beam and the deck is needed. This could be
achieved by a thorough assembling of the connection or by moulding out the gap between the
two elements.
The maximum bending moment at the end of the deck elements in the span direction, My, is
found from FEM-Design to be −502kNm/m, where the moment of the whole deck, Mdeck,y, is
−758kNm. This is found by the same assumptions as for the maximum principal moment. In
the xed connection the moment capacity has to be sucient as well as the anchorage length.
The xed supports is constructed with 12 ø20 steel bars for each deck element with a width of
2.4m. This gives a distance between each reinforcement bar of 200mm. The moment capacity
for negative moment at the xed support, Mend, is in Appendix F given as −1091kNm, which
is greater than the maximum moment and therefore sucient. In Figure 15 the grooves use
to construct the xed supports are shown:
26
==PAGE:34==
Figure 15: Grooves use to construct the xed support between the deck element and the beam
The tensile stresses from the moment needs to be calculated in order to determine the an-
chorage length needed for the support. The full calculation for the stresses and the required
anchorage length is performed through Matlab in Appendix V.
The maximum tensile stress is determined by us of Navier's formula:
σ = N
A + M
I · y
(41)
The only normal force in the cross section is from the pre-tensioned strands. This normal
force contributes with a total of 2.05MPa tensile stress in the top according to Appendix F.
In order to dene the neutral axis of the cross section, the compressions zone in the bottom
concrete needs to be dened. A moment equilibrium around the neutral axis is used to dene
the compression zone and in the tension zone only the slack reinforcement has a contribution
as the concrete is assumed cracked. The equilibrium is given in Equation 42 for x being the
height of the compression zone:
αe · As · (h −x −ds) = Ac · x
2
(42)
Where αe is the relation ES
Ec = 5.24, the area of the slack reinforcement, As, is 3.8 · 10−3m2
and the area of the concrete in the compression zone, Ac is given as b · x. The cover layer of
the slack reinforcement should be at least 25mm and therefore the distance from the center of
the slack reinforcement to the beam end should be ds = 35mm. By solving the equation the
height of the compression zone is found to be 0.072m.
The moment of inertia is a combination of the moment of inertia of the slack reinforcement
and the concrete in the compression zone, given by Equation 43 and Equation 44:

The tension stress in the slack reinforcement, σs, is then found from Equation 41 to be

are added, which gives a σmax of 482.1MPa.
The tension stresses in the slack reinforcement should be less than their design yield strength,
which is fyd = 482.5MPa. The maximum tension stresses is only 0.1% below the yield stress
and therefore almost fully utilized.

6
The beams
The beams will be discussed in this section including the static model from Pre-stress, the
dimensioning criteria for the beam and how the dimensions were found from these.
6.1
Critical beams
A variety of beams could be produced to accompany the combinations of loads as a result of
the building containing several voids. Two continuous beam sections were designed with their
loads applied instead of making a design for each beam. The proles are a continuous beam
which is cantilevered on the one side and a continuous inverted T-beam. Both are simply
supported at ve points and 46.5m long.
(a) Sketch of the regular beam
(b) Sketch of the cantilever beam
Figure 16: The two types of beams
In Figure 16a an inverted T-prole is used as the super-light decks allows for a blade connec-
tion. The beam shown in Figure 16b is used for the edge of the building as it only cantilevers
2.4m from the center of the column to the edge. This also serves as a counterweight to the
torsional moment which will inevitable be generated from the deck span on the opposite side
of the beam.
6.2
Determining the cross sections
The entire prole of 46.5m is divided into nine sections which consist of two dierent types of
beams. This is to optimize it according to both the positive and negative bending moments.
The initial dimensioning of the cross-sections is done through the Matlab script seen in Ap-
pendix W. This script is testing the possible cross sections within the parameters of the width,
height, reinforcement (placement and cross-sectional area) and pre-tensions (placement and
cross sectional area). The parameters are by default limited due to calculation time and as
Matlab has limits on the size of its matrices. This can however be changed by the user. The
script checks for the largest positive and negative bending moment, along with the situation
of the bending moment being 0kNm for both the proles. The two dierent set of proles are
then matched based on the outer dimensions, as it would otherwise lead to the deck elements
having dierent lengths. The possible useful proles (both positive and negative) are then
gathered in a table along with their values for: stress at dierent places of the beam, the
29
==PAGE:37==
prole design, the tension/compression zones, weights, moment of inertia, where there are
cracks if any, the width of these cracks, the diameter of the reinforcement bars used in the
tension zone and the tensile degree of reinforcement (ω).
Once the multiple prole candidates has been found, a single one, in regards to the positive
and negative bending moment, is to be selected for further calculations. These calculations
were mainly done in PRE-stress from Strusoft. The Matlab script did not account for the
shear and thus was calculated separately in Appendix X-Y.
Determining the longitudinal reinforcement and the moment load bearing capacity
The Matlab script, as mentioned in Section 6.2, is designed to give initial dimensions for the
beam sections. As a great variety of combinations of reinforcement, pre-tension and outer
dimensions could be made. Several loops were made in Matlab to account for these variables.

Determining the crack width
The calculation for the width of a crack was also introduced to the script. This was done so
the script could fully account for any cracks made by the pre-tension in combination with the
load. This is done by assuming the minimum and maximum bending moments of both parts
of the beam and calculate it in accordance with [DS/EN 1992-1-1, 2008, Section 7.3.4]:
wk = sr,max (ϵsm −ϵcm)
(62)
Where ϵsm −ϵcm is the dierence of the mean strain in the reinforcement and the mean strain
in the concrete between cracks. This can be calculated as:
ϵsm −ϵcm =
σs −kt

the ratio of Young's modulus, αe, which is 5.53 and Young's modulus for the reinforcement,
Es, which is 210GPa. fct,eff, the eective tension strength of the concrete, is given as:
fct,eff = fctm · βα
cc = fctm · es·(1−(28/t)(1/2))·α

Here t is how many days into the hardening process the concrete is, s a constant dependent
on the type of concrete, which was set to 0.2 according to [DS/EN 1992-1-1, 2008, page 28]
and α an empirical value which is 1 if t < 28 days and 2/3 if t ≥28 days according to
[DS/EN 1992-1-1, 2008, page 28].

respectively. øl is the longitudinal reinforcement and as the script does not calculate the actual
reinforcement bars used, it does instead loop around the standard proles for reinforcement
bars. As wk increases øl increases and the bars used should therefore not exceed the ones used
for the calculations as:
lim

(66)
As the crack width is calculated to ensure that the reinforcement wont corrode, a maximum
allowed width is based on the environment in which it is present. This was done by using
[DS/EN 1992-1-1, 2008, table 7.1N] and was found to be wmax = 0.3 mm, for class XC3 of
[DS/EN 1992-1-1, 2008, table 4.1].

one mentioned as equations in this section.

Determining the dimensioning loads
In order to make the calculations outlined in Section 6.2-6.2, certain loads has to be deter-
mined. These loads were partially provided from PRE-stress by assuming a default uniform
load based on Section 3 and an assumed size of the beam. The load in question was then put
into PRE-stress and the bending moments generated were used in Appendix W. The process
would then repeat as an iteration if the script gave another dimension than the one assumed.
This manual iteration could have been saved by using auxiliary forces and creating a stat-
ically determined structure. The iteration was however assumed to be less time consuming
compared to applying it to the script.
PRE-stress only works in two dimensions which means that it could be used for shear forces and
bending moments while Appendix U were used for the torsional moment. The outer dimensions
of the beams are all inuenced by these three factors and a complicated iteration could have
been possible. The bending moment was however noticed to be the signicant problem for
the beams dimensions and the iteration was therefore based around this and later checked if
the concrete was sucient for the shear and torsion. In order for PRE-stress to calculate the
loads, two ultimate limit state load cases had to be made from [DS/EN 1990, 2007, equation
6.10a-6.10b]:
X
j≥1
γG,j · Gk,j · KFI ” + ” γQ,1ψ0,1 · Qk,1 · KFI
(85)
X
j≥1
ξj · γG,j · Gk,j · KFI ” + ” γQ,1 · Qk,1 · KFI
(86)
Equation 85-86 are simplied version of the ones in [DS/EN 1990, 2007], as the calculations
only contains dead load and live load. It also has the KFI-factor multiplied, as this takes
the consequence class into account. [Jensen og Olsen, 2011, table 4.3] was used for the service
limit state with the live load being often reoccurring and thereby using ψ1,1. The topping of
36
==PAGE:44==
10mm is added as load in PRE-stress and thereby accounted for in the bending moment but
was however not accounted for as a part of the beam and thereby benecial for the beam. It
should also be noted that the density of the beam was assumed to be 2300
kg
m3 rather than a
more accurate one accounting for the actual reinforcement used in the prole.
6.3
Verication of the static model from PRE-stress
The model in PRE-stress is a continuous beam which is simply supported by ve columns.
Such a model is only useful if parts of it can be veried and thereby considered reliable as a
whole. To simplify the model, two of the support columns were removed and the cantilevers at
the ends were given the same length. This made the model symmetrical and with the length
between columns of l1 = 21.6m and a cantilever par at the end of l2 = 0.9m.
Figure 18: The simplied model in PRE-stress for verication
The moment at mid-point was used for verication. The model have three vertical supports
and only two equations of equilibrium (vertical and zero moment in one of the ends) which
therefore required the use of virtual work in the form of auxiliary forces.
This was done
using the integration formulas from [Jensen og Olsen, 2011, page 100]. The calculations are
seen in Appendix Z where the moment at midpoint is found to be -1165kNm which is 3kNm
(0.26%) lower than the value from PRE-stress of -1162kNm. This error can be explained by
the assumption, mentioned in Appendix Z, that the moment at support A is 0kNm when
integrating the moment curves from support A to support B.
6.4
The beam prole
A connection is in the beam is needed as the prole is divided into multiple sections. This
is made as a blade connection similar to how the deck and beam is connected. As such a
connection is highly dependant on its ability to transfer the moment, the connections are
made where this is 0 kNm according to the calculations. This can not be guaranteed due to
the live load and such a connection should be designed to transfer an amount higher than
0 kNm. This connection has however not been a part of the scope of this project and it is
simply assumed that it can be made suciently strong.
6.5
The nal proles
The dimensions of the nal prole was assembled by using Section 6.2-6.2 and loads from
PRE-stress. Through the iteration process described in Section 6.2.

43
==PAGE:51==
with positive bending moment. This was pushed to the limit to minimize the deformations
of the oor structure even though it was nowhere near its allowable limit of 36mm. MEd is
also close to its respectable limit for the part with negative bending moment. This could be
lowered by either adding more pre-tension or reinforcement in the top part of the beam. If the
pre-tension was chosen, σp0 should be lowered for the part as the current pre-tension already
gives a negative (meaning upwards) deformation of the deck. This deformation is found at
one of the cantilever parts. It is also seen that the maximum value for σc, in the negative
bending moment part, was found in the release stage which is a short term stage. PRE-stress
does however not strike out on large compression forces for short term even though they
are visible through the tables presented in the program. Both values of σc in Table 17 are
in the early stages of the structure and precautions should be taken as the concrete wont
have reached its full strength at the given time. Equation 64 can be used to calculate the
compressive strength at day t by assuming the hardening process for tensile and compressive
strength are aligned. [DS/EN 1992-1-1, 2008, Table 3.1] gives the target values by converting
the characteristic strength into design strength and nd the equivalent mean tensile strength.
The target values are:
fck = σc · γc = −33.4 MPa and −44.5 MPa
(87)
These values for the compressive strength are found when fct,eff is approximately 3.2MPa
and 3.8MPa. By rewriting Equation 64, βcc can be isolated as:
βcc(t) =
fcm
fcm
(1/α)
(88)
t can then be isolated from the equation which leads to a necessary maturing of 5 and 13
days respectively before applying the pre-tension to the beam. This will lead to the beam
taking up a lot of space in the production facility which could eventually overrun the cost of
reducing the pre-tension and adding more reinforcement instead and the cost of taking up the
space should therefore denitely be looked into. This was however not done as the economy
of the construction is not a direct part of this project. Another thing to be aware of when
adding maturing days, is the lowering in the creep factor. Lowering the creep factor will lead
to less deformations which can have a negative eect on the stresses during the early stages
of the maturing process. This is due to the deformation being towards the opposite side of
the pre-tension and an increased deformation will thereby lower the eect of the pre-tensioned
strands as they come into compression. Lastly the shear capacity is missing from the table as
PRE-stress only allows for one stirrup and when redesigning the correct amount of stirrups to
one equivalent it would exceed a limitation within the program which caused it to not account
for the stirrups. The calculations made in Appendix X-Y are therefore the nal ones.

In this section the initial dimensions of the beam proles, according to the maximum moment,
were designed and later tested in PRE-stress. The proles were set to have a height of 470mm
with 10 mm topping added. Such a height lead to the necessary outer dimensions of the
inverted T-beam to be a lower part of 2100mm x 320mm with a top of 1700mm x 150mm.
The width of the cantilever was sucient at its minimum which lead to a lower part of 2600mm
x 320mm with a top of 2400mm x 150mm. It was seen that the FEM calculation of PRE-stress
allowed for less reinforcement and pre-tensioning to be used. This lead to a reduction in the
inverted T-beam of 1663mm2 and 4127mm2 reinforcement steel for the postive and negative
bending moment respectively. A reduction of 2400mm2 and 5495mm2 was shown respectively
for the cantilever beam in the positive and negative bending moment part. The pre-tensioning
was reduced by 200mm2 for the negative bending moment of the cantilever beam while the
others only had insignicant changes due to the pre-tensioning strands having to t with
46
==PAGE:54==
standard proles. A conservative calculation for the shear and torsional moment was done by
assuming the shear was constant at its maximum for the beam part and the torsion was found
as the average of the most sever meter of the entire 46.5m span of the beam type. This lead
to a shear reinforcement for the inverted T-beam of 6ø12/150 and 7ø14/150, respectively, for
the positive and negative bending. For the cantilevered beam 4ø10/200 and 5ø12/200 were
used for the positive and negative bending moment respectively. For the torsion it was found
to require 1ø6/150 for both the inverted T-beams while the cantilevered ones had 1ø6/200.
This was the case as ø6 is the smallest standard dimension of the strands and was sucient at
the worst parts of the beams and the only dierence is the distance which was based on the
distance between the shear stirrups. The utilization of the concrete, in shear and torsion, was
also found to be below 50% for the inverted T-beam and below 8% for the cantilevered part.
This could be optimized by reducing the area covered by the shear and torsional reinforcement
and thereby use less of concrete to take the compression generated from these forces. The
shear reinforcement was found to have a utilization below than 24% for the inverted T-beams
and below 86% for the cantilevered ones. In PRE-stress it was found that for the inverted
T-beam it was necessary to let the concrete cure for 5 days for the positive bending moment
part and 13 days for the negative bending moment part in order to not exceed the compression
stresses. Such a precaution was not need for the cantilevered beam. In PRE-stress the pre-
tensioning was pushed towards its limit which lead to a utilization of 95% for the inverted
T-beam with positive bending moment and 83% for the one with negative bending moment.
It was not possible to nd the utilization of the pre-tensioning for the cantilevered beam with
positive bending moment due to its placement. The part with negative bending moment was
however at 90%. All the beams had crack widths below 44% of the allowed, apart from the
cantilevered beam in positive bending moment as this reached 87%. All parts of the beams
had a maximum deection lower than 22mm (60%) in the service limit state. This all lead to
the prole having sucient capacity even though it was pushed to its limit at several points.

==PAGE:55==
7
Problems with low beams
Several things has to be taken into consideration when designing load bearing beams. One
thing that should be considered is the height of the beam. Through this project, it has been
a goal to have the height equal to the decks as this would create the most aesthetic look. The
eects of a change in height will be looked into in this section, as it is the only set value for
the beams.
σc = M
Ii
· y + N
Ai
(89)
Looking at Navier's formula (Equation 89), the bending moment will slightly change due to
the increased cross section of the beam and hence the weight. The distance from the neutral
axis, y, to the outer parts of the prole (top and bottom part for an uncracked prole) will
also increase as a function of the height. The ideal moment of inertia, Ii, will however govern
the stresses from increasing for a prole of the size used in this part. This is due to Ii having
the distance of the members squared and the height cubed.
Figure 27:
y
Ii for both the top, zit, and bottom, zib, part of an uncracked section
As seen in Figure 27, the change in the factor y
Ii can be described as a hyperbolic function for
this prole. It is seen that the factor is approximately lowered by 50% when the beam height
is increased by 200mm for the tension zone and 220mm for the pressure zone.
48
==PAGE:56==
Figure 28: The maximum bending moment from load as a function of the beams height
It is seen from Figure 28 that the bending moment increase is a linear function of the beam
height. The increase in the bending moment is unfavorable for the beam but at the design
height of 0.47m the beams dead load accounts for 12.85% (17.753kN/m) of the total load
of 84.132kN/m dead load and 54kN/m live load. By increasing the beam to 1m, from the
designed 0.47m, the bending moment only has a 12% increase.
Figure 29: The stresses from the load as a function of the height
In Figure 29 it is seen that the stresses from the load, in the cross section, are decreasing as
the height increases due to the factor in Figure 27 decreasing faster than the bending moment
in Figure 28.
The pre-tension has however not been accounted for. The pre-tension consists of a bending
moment and an axial force in Equation 89.
The stresses from the bending moment will,
to some extend, react in the same fashion as for the load when the height increases. The
stresses from the bending moment increases as the height increase while it decreases as
y
Ii
decreases.
The normal force is assumed constant and the stresses will therefore decrease
49
==PAGE:57==
inversely proportionally as the ideal cross sectional area, Ai, increases.
Figure 30: The stress from the normal force as a function of the height
The stresses from the normal force are decreasing in Figure 30 as the ideal cross sectional
area continuously increases and the normal force is constant. This is a disadvantage for the
bearing capacity of an uncracked beam as the weakest part is likely to be the tension zone.
Figure 31: The stress from the moment generated by the pre-tension as a function of the height
The inuence from the bending moment, seen in Figure 31, is a hyperbolic function like
Figure 27. The bending moment from the pre-tension is described as M0 = P0 · zip (zip ≈zib
being the distance from the pre-tension to the neutral axis) and even though zip increases, so
does the ideal moment of inertia, which outweighs the eect as previously described.
50
==PAGE:58==
Figure 32: The combined stresses from the pre-tension as a function of the height
The benets of the pre-tension is clear when combining the normal force and moment of the
pre-tension in Figure 32. The bottom part is aected by large compression stresses which
counteracts parts of the tensile stresses while the top part has increased tensile stresses which
again counteracts the stresses from the load. If the prole height were to increase, so should
the pre-tension, as its eect will decrease compared to its current eect. This relative decrease
is due to Ii having the distance of the members squared and the height cubed, as explained
earlier, while the lever arm increases by less than the increase in height.
Figure 33: The total stresses as a function of the height
The change in stresses as a function of the height is seen in Figure 33 along side the stresses
from the load. The clear benet on the stresses from the pre-tension is to be found in the
tension zone. Like previously seen, this decreases as a hyperbolic function.
51
==PAGE:59==
It was seen in this section that xing the height of a beam, based on the minimum of the
deck, has a high impact on the stresses in the beam. The impact of the pre-tensioning was
also shown to be highly related to the height of the cross section due to the lever arm and
moment of inertia increasing. More pre-tensioning could be added by changing the height of
the prole and thereby increasing the impact it has on the beam.
8
Ventilation ducts in the super-light concrete deck
An analysis investigating the eect of ventilation ducts in the deck elements is performed in
Abaqus. A deck element without a duct is compared to deck elements with a ventilation duct
at dierent locations and to ventilation ducts in the middle with dierent sizes and shapes.
To compare the eect of ventilations ducts both deformations, stresses and frequencies are
investigated.
Before an analysis is performed in Abaqus it is veried that the parameters from Abaqus is
determined correctly. Both deformations, stresses and frequencies are veried.
8.1
Verication of Abaqus
In the verication of the deformation and stresses, a simply supported concrete beam is used.
The beams has a length of 10m, a quadratic cross section with a width and height is 0.5m
with a Young's modulus of 40GPa. The moment of inertia is then 5.208 · 10−3m4 and the
beam is loaded with a line load of 10kN/m.
The maximum analytical deformation is given from Equation 38 to 6.25mm. The maximum
stress is determined from Navier's formula, Equation 41, where the normal forces is zero due
to pure bending in the beam.
As the cross section of the beam being double symmetric, the height y is half of the cross
section height and the maximum moment is given from Equation 37 as 1.25 · 105Nm. The
maximum stress, σmax in the beam is then 6.00MPa.
In Abaqus the beam is modelled as a wire with a prole assigned.
The model including
deformation is shown in Figure 34:
Figure 34: Beam modelled as a wire with colors showing deformations
Both the largest deformation and stress are in the middle of the beam and are 6.29mm and
52
==PAGE:60==
6.00MPa respectively. The deviation from the analytical results are 0.0% for the stress and
0.6% for the deformation and the two parameters are then assumed veried.
Appendix F is used for comparison in the verication of the frequencies. In Appendix F a
deck of the same type as used in Section 4.1, with a width of 2.4m, a length of 10.8m and
with the thickness changed to 480mm, is calculated. The light concrete blocks in the deck
are 430mm heigh and frequencies for three dierent supports are investigated.
The three
supports investigated are simple-simple, simple-xed and xed-xed. In Abaqus the deck is
modelled consisting of both light concrete blocks, normal concrete and reinforcement strands
with dierent material properties. Simple supports are made in the bottom of the beam end
while xed supports are applied to the entire beam end where needed. The Abaqus model is
shown in Figure 35:
Figure 35: Super-light deck element modelled in Abaqus with dierent types of materials
The frequencies are listed in Table 19 for the three dierent support conditions for both
Appendix F and the Abaqus analysis along with the deviation between them:

The maximum deviations between Appendix F and Abaqus is 1.5% which is acceptable. The

is hard be certain of the reason for it.

==PAGE:61==
8.2
Analysis in Abaqus
In the analysis the results of each deck element has been checked with a ner mesh and no
signicant change in the results has been observed.
Ventilation ducts at dierent locations
An investigation of the eect of making ducts in the deck elements to ventilation shafts are
performed with help from Abaqus. A simply supported deck is modelled in Abaqus and four
dierent models with a ventilation duct are constructed from this model. One with a duct in
the middle of the deck, called mid-center, one with a duct at the edge of the deck but in the
middle between the supports, called mid-edge, one in center of the deck at the support in one
end, called end-center and at last one at the edge of the deck at the support in one end, called
end-edge.
Martin Buhl from Rambøll, has estimated roughly that if the whole building has laboratories
four quadratic ventilation shafts of ø1400 are sucient and if only one oor has laboratories
three circular shafts of ø900 would be sucient. He has estimated that a realistic size of the
ventilation in the building would be four quadratic ventilation shafts of ø1000. The model is
created with a quadratic duct with width and length of 1000mm.
The deck size in the model is the same as the one used in the frequency analysis verication.
The load applied in the model is a combination of the live load and the weight of the deck and
is applied as a pressure load, q, of 12500N/m2. The Young's moduli used in the analysis are
Ec = 38GPa, Es = 195GPa and EcL = 2.1GPa. For the dynamic analsysis used to determine
the frequency of the rst mode shape the Young's modulus is multiplied by 1.1 to get the
dynamic Young's modulus.
The results from the analysis can not be used directly for the whole deck, as it is performed for
simply supported single deck element, but it could indicate where to be cautious in relation
to the ventilation ducts.
In Figure 36 the deformation shape of the element without a ventilation duct is shown, which
will not change shape when the ventilations ducts are constructed. The stress distribution are
not easily seen in Abaqus, as the largest stresses are inside the element in the reinforcement.
In Figure 37 the stress distribution for a deck with a quadratic duct ø1400 is shown.
In
Appendix AD all deformation shapes and stress distributions from the analysis are shown
together with the analysis data for each case.

The deformation is increased if a ventilation duct is constructed in the deck element. The

deformation peaks compared to weakening at the support where it is less critical.

not in the center. This is due to a unsymmetric distribution of the force along the deck which

If ventilation ducts are constructed in the deck element the tension stresses in the strong
concrete increases. If the duct is located at the support the increase would be more than 17%
and if the duct is located at the center of the deck the tension stresses are increased more
than 90%. The tension stresses in the deck is largest in the middle and therefore a ventilation
duct located at this point will therefore give the largest increase in stresses. The ventilation
duct would remove a great part of the strong concrete and therefore drastically increases the
stresses in the rest of the concrete as the maximum bending moment is also located at this
point. The duct placed at the support of the deck weaken the deck in a less critical point and
therefore gives a smaller increase in stresses.
No clear relation between the tension stresses has been observed in relation to moving the
duct from the middle towards the edge of the deck.

ø400, ø600, ø750, ø1000, ø1100 and ø1400 and the four circular ducts have the sizes, ø400,
ø750, ø1000, ø1200. These are all compared to a deck element without a ventilation duct.

together with the analysis data for each case.

deformation of the deck.

9
Alternative solutions
In the project a static solution was chosen with super-light decks xed to wide beams, of the
same height as the decks, which are then constructed above each column row. This solution
is chosen in order to get the largest possible height at each oor and at the same time have all
the decks and beams at each oor act like a continuous disc. The building is cantilevered in
two directions and therefore an alternative solution to the direction of the decks spanning was
needed. The chosen solution was to make the wide beams in the construction wide enough to
cover the cantilevered span.
Other solutions were also considered in the early stages of the project. A beam solution close
to the chosen one could be beams that are still connected to the decks by xed supports in the
deck level, but with a greater height than the decks. This solution would give more narrow
but higher beams and by doing so a greater part of the disc would be made of super-light
concrete. The greater height of the beams would however give a lower oor height.
Another solution which have the same advantages and disadvantages as the solution with a
more narrow and higher beam than the one used in the project is a solution where the beams
are placed beneath the super-light decks. This would give simply supported decks on the
beams. The simply supported oor disc would then have a greater deformation and would
not take advantage of the deck-beam connection which is currently used.
If the beams are not made wide enough to cover the entire span of cantilevered part of the
decks spanning direction, another solution should be considered. The cantilevered part could
be constructed with inlaid beams in the super-light deck elements. A row of light concrete
blocks would be replaced by a reinforced concrete beam. This solution has the advantage that
the height of the cross section would not be increased by an inlaid beam.
Another solution to the cantilever part of the decks, in the decks spanning direction, could
be to construct a beam spanning in two directions at the ends of the building to get deck
elements supported in their spanning direction at the cantilevered ends.
The beams could also have been constructed as steel proles instead of the concrete used for
this project. The steel proles could also be cast into the concrete beam for additional stiness
like a Delta beam from Peikko. A way to avoid a lower oor height even if the beams are
increase could be by leading the installations through the beams instead of under assuming
the building has suspended ceiling, which is most likely.
63
==PAGE:71==
10
Project design compared to the design from MOE
MOE have in their building design chosen to use bubble deck with a thickness of 450mm. The
building design in the project has been modied quite signicant since the projects beginning
and therefore the building data from MOE is given for a dierent building design. Most of
the comparable parameters are on the other hand still based on the same. The span is 10.8m
x 10.8m and the load aecting each oor is still 5kN/m2. The plate moments for the 3rd oor
from the design MOE has calculated is given in Figure 44 for ultimate limit state for both the
x- and y-direction of the deck span.
Figure 44: 3rd oor showing maximum plate moments at ultimate limit state for both the x- and
y-direction for bubble deck
The moments are in Figure 44 only considered between the columns instead of at the supports.
At the column supports the deck is constructed as a massive concrete deck and is therefore
not relevant in designing the bubble deck part of the oor. The maximum plate moment is
291kNm/m in the x-direction and 284kNm/m in the y-direction for the 3rd oor.
The plate moments are for MOE's project building used to determine the size of the bubble
decks reinforcement grid. The moments used for this are however the moments from the 6th
oor which does not exist in the preliminary design that the project is based on and there-
fore not comparable. The reinforcement grid is dimensioned to ø20 c/c 100 from Appendix AE.

The frequency for the rst mode shape of the bubble deck is given by Figure 47 to 7.7Hz

Figure 47: First mode shape and related frequency
The lowest frequency of the oor of super-light deck elements is 8.4Hz which is 9% higher
than for the bubble deck, even though the frequency of the super-light oor has used a very
conservative resonant mass in the frequency calculation. A more accurate frequency with a
more realistic resonant mass is calculated to 10.0Hz which is 30% higher than the frequency
for the bubble deck oor. Instead of increasing the frequency in the calculation with a more
realistic resonant mass on the super-light deck elements the height could be decreased. It
would result in a height of the deck on 390mm and a frequency of 8.1Hz. The frequency
would then still be 6% higher compared to the bubble deck and the height of the cross section
would at the same time be 60mm lower.
In Appendix AI it is describe how the frequency of the bubble deck could be increased by
adding non load carrying columns to support the decks. If columns of the type HUP 100-100-
6,3 is used an increase in the frequency of 1 −1.5Hz could be obtained. If a further increase
is needed a massive 100mm x 100mm steel column would give an increase of 2 −2.5Hz. The
columns has to be anchored in the base plate in order to have the described eect.
68
==PAGE:76==
The super-light deck in the project is 30mm larger than the bubble deck designed by MOE
while the deformations of the super-light oor is 33% smaller than the bubble decks. Di-
mensioning the cross section size are not described for the bubble deck, but the super-light
deck is dimensioned according to the frequency criteria of 8Hz. In the project the super-light
deck has a frequency of 8.4Hz but using a realistic resonant mass this would be increased to
10.0Hz. The frequency for the bubble deck is 7.7Hz which is lower than the required 8Hz and
therefore the bubble deck needs to be reinforced by non load bearing columns. If a realistic
resonant mass is used in the project the super-light deck could have a cross section height
of 390mm and a frequency of 8.1Hz, but the moment capacity of this is not checked. The
moment capacity of the 480mm deck have however free capacity to lower the cross section
height so it would be realistic to get a lower height and higher frequency of the super-light
deck element compared to the bubble deck. The density of the two deck types have been
dicult to compare because of the uncertainties in the oor design of the bubble deck around
columns. The density does however seem to be close to the same for the two element types
and the amount of reinforcement is smaller in the super-light deck compared to the bubble
decks.
